<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Alcoholism</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Problematic excessive alcohol consumption</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about chronic alcohol abuse that results in significant health problems. For alcohol abuse in general, see <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_abuse" title="Alcohol abuse">Alcohol abuse</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Alcoholic" redirects here. For alcoholic beverages, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_drink" title="Alcoholic drink">Alcoholic drink</a>. For alcohol consumption by people in general, see <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">Alcohol (drug)</a>. For the chemical compound in general, see <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">Alcohol (chemistry)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIHHx_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIHHx-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Duration</th><td class="infobox-data">Long term<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013_2-1"><a href="/wiki/Acamprosate" title="Acamprosate">acamprosate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">disulfiram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone" title="Naltrexone">naltrexone</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan-Lopez-2006_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-Lopez-2006-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Test2014_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Test2014-10">[10]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">380 million / 5.1% adults (2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un2020_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-un2020-12">[12]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">3.3 million / 5.9%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Stats_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Stats-13">[13]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Alcoholism</b> is, broadly, any drinking of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">alcohol</a> that results in significant <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental</a> or physical <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Because there is disagreement on the definition of the word <i>alcoholism</i>, it is not a recognized diagnostic entity. Predominant diagnostic classifications are <b>alcohol use disorder</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013-2">[2]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dependence" title="Alcohol dependence">alcohol dependence</a> (<a href="/wiki/ICD-11" title="ICD-11">ICD-11</a>); these are defined in their respective sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Excessive alcohol use can damage all organ systems, but it particularly affects the brain, heart, liver, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIHHx_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIHHx-5">[5]</a></sup> Alcoholism can result in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wernicke%E2%80%93Korsakoff_syndrome" title="Wernicke–Korsakoff syndrome">Wernicke–Korsakoff syndrome</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">irregular heartbeat</a>, an impaired immune response, liver <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_and_cancer" title="Alcohol and cancer">increased cancer risk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIHHx_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIHHx-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Drinking during <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> can result in <a href="/wiki/Fetal_alcohol_spectrum_disorder" title="Fetal alcohol spectrum disorder">fetal alcohol spectrum disorders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAS2015_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAS2015-3">[3]</a></sup> Women are generally more sensitive than men to the harmful effects of alcohol, primarily due to their smaller body weight, lower capacity to metabolize alcohol, and higher proportion of body fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-11">[11]</a></sup> In a small number of individuals, prolonged, severe alcohol misuse ultimately leads to cognitive impairment and frank <a href="/wiki/Alcohol-related_dementia" title="Alcohol-related dementia">dementia</a>.
</p><p>Environment and genetics are two factors in the risk of development of alcoholism, with about half the risk attributed to each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Stress and associated disorders, including anxiety, are key factors in the development of alcoholism as alcohol consumption can temporarily reduce dysphoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moonat-Pandey-2012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moonat-Pandey-2012-17">[17]</a></sup> Someone with a parent or sibling with an alcohol use disorder is three to four times more likely to develop an alcohol use disorder themselves, but only a minority of them do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Environmental factors include social, cultural and behavioral influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agarwal-Kozlowski-2000_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agarwal-Kozlowski-2000-18">[18]</a></sup> High <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(biological)" title="Stress (biological)">stress levels</a> and anxiety, as well as alcohol's inexpensive cost and easy accessibility, increase the risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon2012_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moon2012-7">[7]</a></sup> People may continue to drink partly to prevent or improve symptoms of withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> After a person stops drinking alcohol, they may experience a low level of withdrawal lasting for months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Medically, alcoholism is considered both a physical and mental illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Questionnaires are usually used to detect possible alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Further information is then collected to confirm the diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prevention of alcoholism may be attempted by reducing the experience of stress and anxiety in individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon2012_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moon2012-7">[7]</a></sup> It can be attempted by regulating and limiting the sale of alcohol (particularly to minors), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_tax" title="Alcohol tax">taxing alcohol</a> to increase its cost, and providing education and treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatment of alcoholism may take several forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup> Due to medical problems that can occur during withdrawal, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_detoxification" title="Alcohol detoxification">alcohol cessation</a> should be controlled carefully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup> One common method involves the use of <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepine</a> medications, such as <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">diazepam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup> These can be taken while admitted to a health care institution or individually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup> The medications <a href="/wiki/Acamprosate" title="Acamprosate">acamprosate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">disulfiram</a> or <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone" title="Naltrexone">naltrexone</a> may also be used to help prevent further drinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Test2014_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Test2014-10">[10]</a></sup> Mental illness or other <a href="/wiki/Addiction" title="Addiction">addictions</a> may complicate treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Various forms of individual or group therapy or <a href="/wiki/Support_group" title="Support group">support groups</a> are used to attempt to keep a person from returning to alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan-Lopez-2006_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-Lopez-2006-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> One support group is <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> has estimated that as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alcoholism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 380 million people with alcoholism worldwide (5.1% of the population over 15 years of age).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un2020_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-un2020-12">[12]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alcoholism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> in the United States, about 17 million (7%) of adults and 0.7 million (2.8%) of those age 12 to 17 years of age are affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Stats_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Stats-13">[13]</a></sup> Alcoholism is most common among males and young adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Geographically, it is least common in Africa (1.1% of the population) and has the highest rates in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> (11%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Alcoholism directly resulted in 139,000 deaths in 2013, up from 112,000 deaths in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-26">[26]</a></sup> A total of 3.3 million deaths (5.9% of all deaths) are believed to be due to alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Stats_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Stats-13">[13]</a></sup> Alcoholism reduces a person's <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> by approximately ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schu2014_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schu2014-27">[27]</a></sup> Many terms, some <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">slurs</a> and others <a href="/wiki/Slang" title="Slang">informal</a>, have been used to refer to people affected by alcoholism; the expressions include <i>tippler</i>, <i>drunkard</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipsomaniac" title="Dipsomaniac">dipsomaniac</a></i> and <i>souse</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1979, the World Health Organization discouraged the use of <i>alcoholism</i> due to its inexact meaning, preferring <i>alcohol dependence syndrome</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ladtpwho_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ladtpwho-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<p>The risk of alcohol dependence begins at low levels of drinking and increases directly with both the volume of alcohol consumed and a pattern of <a href="/wiki/Binge_drinking" title="Binge drinking">drinking larger amounts on an occasion</a>, to the point of intoxication, which is sometimes called <i>binge drinking</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_misuse">Long-term misuse</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1485" data-file-width="2079" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg/350px-Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg/525px-Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg/700px-Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Possible_long-term_effects_of_ethanol.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some of the possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-term_effects_of_ethanol" title="Long-term effects of ethanol">long-term effects of ethanol</a> an individual may develop. Additionally, in pregnant women, alcohol can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetal_alcohol_syndrome" title="Fetal alcohol syndrome">fetal alcohol syndrome</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alcoholism is characterised by an increased <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_tolerance" title="Alcohol tolerance">tolerance</a> to alcohol – which means that an individual can consume more alcohol – and <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a> on alcohol, which makes it hard for an individual to control their consumption. The physical dependency caused by alcohol can lead to an affected individual having a very strong urge to drink alcohol. These characteristics play a role in decreasing the ability to stop drinking of an individual with an alcohol use disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman-1996_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-1996-30">[30]</a></sup> Alcoholism can have adverse effects on mental health, contributing to psychiatric disorders and increasing the risk of suicide. A <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depressed mood</a> is a common symptom of heavy alcohol drinkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drug_abuse_prevention_a03_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-drug_abuse_prevention_a03-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warning_signs">Warning signs</span></h3>
<p>Warning signs of alcoholism include the consumption of increasing amounts of alcohol and frequent intoxication, preoccupation with drinking to the exclusion of other activities, promises to quit drinking and failure to keep those promises, the inability to remember what was said or done while drinking (colloquially known as "blackouts"), personality changes associated with drinking, denial or the making of excuses for drinking, the refusal to admit excessive drinking, dysfunction or other problems at work or school, the loss of interest in personal appearance or hygiene, marital and economic problems, and the complaint of poor health, with loss of appetite, respiratory infections, or increased anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Short-term_effects">Short-term effects</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Short-term_effects_of_alcohol_consumption" title="Short-term effects of alcohol consumption">Short-term effects of alcohol consumption</a></div>
<p>Drinking enough to cause a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_alcohol_concentration" title="Blood alcohol concentration">blood alcohol concentration</a> (BAC) of 0.03–0.12% typically causes an overall improvement in mood and possible <a href="/wiki/Euphoria" title="Euphoria">euphoria</a> (a "happy" feeling), increased self-confidence and sociability, decreased anxiety, a <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_flush_reaction" title="Alcohol flush reaction">flushed, red appearance in the face</a> and impaired judgment and fine muscle coordination. A BAC of 0.09% to 0.25% causes <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedation" title="Sedation">sedation</a>, balance problems and blurred vision. A BAC of 0.18% to 0.30% causes profound confusion, impaired speech (e.g. slurred speech), staggering, dizziness and vomiting. A BAC from 0.25% to 0.40% causes <a href="/wiki/Stupor" title="Stupor">stupor</a>, unconsciousness, <a href="/wiki/Anterograde_amnesia" title="Anterograde amnesia">anterograde amnesia</a>, vomiting (death may occur due to <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_aspiration" title="Pulmonary aspiration">inhalation of vomit</a> while unconscious) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_depression" title="Respiratory depression">respiratory depression</a> (potentially life-threatening). A BAC from 0.35% to 0.80% causes a <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> (unconsciousness), life-threatening respiratory depression and possibly fatal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_poisoning" title="Alcohol poisoning">alcohol poisoning</a>. With all alcoholic beverages, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drinking_and_driving" title="Drinking and driving">drinking while driving</a>, operating an aircraft or heavy machinery increases the risk of an accident; many countries have penalties for drunk driving.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_effects">Long-term effects</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-term_effects_of_alcohol_consumption" title="Long-term effects of alcohol consumption">Long-term effects of alcohol consumption</a></div>
<p>Having more than one drink a day for women or two drinks for men increases the risk of heart disease, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atrial_fibrillation" title="Atrial fibrillation">atrial fibrillation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kee2014_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kee2014-34">[34]</a></sup> Risk is greater with <a href="/wiki/Binge_drinking" title="Binge drinking">binge drinking</a>, which may also result in violence or accidents. About 3.3 million deaths (5.9% of all deaths) are believed to be due to alcohol each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Stats_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Stats-13">[13]</a></sup> Alcoholism reduces a person's life expectancy by around ten years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schu2014_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schu2014-27">[27]</a></sup> and alcohol use is the third leading cause of early death in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kee2014_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kee2014-34">[34]</a></sup> Long-term alcohol misuse can cause a number of physical symptoms, including <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> of the liver, <a href="/wiki/Pancreatitis" title="Pancreatitis">pancreatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyneuropathy" title="Polyneuropathy">polyneuropathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wernicke-Korsakoff_syndrome" title="Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome">alcoholic dementia</a>, heart disease, nutritional deficiencies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duodenal_ulcer" title="Duodenal ulcer">peptic ulcers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dysfunction" title="Sexual dysfunction">sexual dysfunction</a>, and can eventually be fatal. Other physical effects include an increased risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malabsorption" title="Malabsorption">malabsorption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">alcoholic liver disease</a>, and several <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a>. Damage to the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> can occur from sustained alcohol consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> A wide range of immunologic defects can result and there may be a generalized skeletal fragility, in addition to a recognized tendency to accidental injury, resulting in a propensity for bone fractures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women develop long-term complications of alcohol dependence more rapidly than do men, women also have a higher mortality rate from alcoholism than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870-39">[39]</a></sup> Examples of long-term complications include brain, heart, and liver damage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walter_H.,_Gutierrez_K.,_Ramskogler_K.,_Hertling_I.,_Dvorak_A.,_Lesch_O.M.,_et_al._2003_253–268_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_H.,_Gutierrez_K.,_Ramskogler_K.,_Hertling_I.,_Dvorak_A.,_Lesch_O.M.,_et_al._2003_253–268-40">[40]</a></sup> and an <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_and_breast_cancer" title="Alcohol and breast cancer">increased risk of breast cancer</a>. Additionally, heavy drinking over time has been found to have a negative effect on reproductive functioning in women. This results in reproductive dysfunction such as <a href="/wiki/Anovulation" title="Anovulation">anovulation</a>, decreased ovarian mass, problems or irregularity of the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a>, and early <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870-39">[39]</a></sup> Alcoholic <a href="/wiki/Ketoacidosis" title="Ketoacidosis">ketoacidosis</a> can occur in individuals who chronically misuse alcohol and have a recent history of <a href="/wiki/Binge_drinking" title="Binge drinking">binge drinking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mihai-_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mihai--41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sibaï-2005_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sibaï-2005-42">[42]</a></sup> The amount of alcohol that can be biologically processed and its effects differ between sexes. Equal dosages of alcohol consumed by men and women generally result in women having higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_alcohol_concentration" title="Blood alcohol concentration">blood alcohol concentrations</a> (BACs), since women generally have a lower weight and higher percentage of body fat and therefore a lower volume of distribution for alcohol than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23101976_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23101976-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychiatric">Psychiatric</span></h4>
<p>Long-term misuse of alcohol can cause a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> problems. Severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> problems are common; approximately 10% of all dementia cases are related to alcohol consumption, making it the second leading cause of <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aamaibnc_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-aamaibnc-44">[44]</a></sup> Excessive alcohol use causes <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">damage to brain function</a>, and psychological health can be increasingly affected over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_skills" title="Social skills">Social skills</a> are significantly impaired in people with alcoholism due to the neurotoxic effects of alcohol on the brain, especially the <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a> area of the brain. The social skills that are impaired by alcohol use disorder include impairments in perceiving facial emotions, <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosody</a>, perception problems, and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">theory of mind</a> deficits; the ability to understand humor is also impaired in people who misuse alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18412750_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18412750-46">[46]</a></sup> Psychiatric disorders are common in people with alcohol use disorders, with as many as 25% also having severe psychiatric disturbances. The most prevalent psychiatric symptoms are <a href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorder" title="Anxiety disorder">anxiety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a> disorders. Psychiatric symptoms usually initially worsen during alcohol withdrawal, but typically improve or disappear with continued abstinence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">Psychosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confusion" title="Confusion">confusion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organic_brain_syndrome" title="Organic brain syndrome">organic brain syndrome</a> may be caused by alcohol misuse, which can lead to a misdiagnosis such as <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Panic_disorder" title="Panic disorder">Panic disorder</a> can develop or worsen as a direct result of long-term alcohol misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The co-occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">major depressive disorder</a> and alcoholism is well documented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Among those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comorbid" title="Comorbid">comorbid</a> occurrences, a distinction is commonly made between depressive episodes that remit with alcohol abstinence ("substance-induced"), and depressive episodes that are primary and do not remit with abstinence ("independent" episodes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Additional use of other drugs may increase the risk of depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Psychiatric disorders differ depending on gender. Women who have alcohol-use disorders often have a co-occurring psychiatric diagnosis such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_depression" title="Major depression">major depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panic_disorder" title="Panic disorder">panic disorder</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulimia" title="Bulimia">bulimia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> (PTSD), or <a href="/wiki/Borderline_personality_disorder" title="Borderline personality disorder">borderline personality disorder</a>. Men with alcohol-use disorders more often have a co-occurring diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Narcissistic_personality_disorder" title="Narcissistic personality disorder">narcissistic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Antisocial_personality_disorder" title="Antisocial personality disorder">antisocial personality disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impulse_disorder" title="Impulse disorder">impulse disorders</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention_deficit/hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder">attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder</a> (ADHD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karrol_2002_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karrol_2002-58">[58]</a></sup> Women with alcohol use disorder are more likely to experience physical or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_assault" title="Sexual assault">sexual assault</a>, abuse, and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a> than women in the general population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karrol_2002_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karrol_2002-58">[58]</a></sup> which can lead to higher instances of psychiatric disorders and greater dependence on alcohol.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_effects">Social effects</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Drug-related_crime" title="Drug-related crime">Drug-related crime</a></div>
<p>Serious social problems arise from alcohol use disorder; these dilemmas are caused by the pathological changes in the brain and the intoxicating effects of alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aamaibnc_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-aamaibnc-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers-59">[59]</a></sup> Alcohol misuse is associated with an increased risk of committing criminal offences, including <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">child abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burglary" title="Burglary">burglary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assault" title="Assault">assault</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drug_use_a_reference_handbook_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-drug_use_a_reference_handbook-60">[60]</a></sup> Alcoholism is associated with <a href="/wiki/Termination_of_employment" title="Termination of employment">loss of employment</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> which can lead to financial problems. Drinking at inappropriate times and behavior caused by reduced judgment can lead to legal consequences, such as criminal charges for <a href="/wiki/Drunk_driving" title="Drunk driving">drunk driving</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abd2009_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-abd2009-62">[62]</a></sup> or public disorder, or civil penalties for <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tortious</a> behavior. An alcoholic's behavior and mental impairment while drunk can profoundly affect those surrounding him and lead to isolation from family and friends. This isolation can lead to <a href="/wiki/Relational_disorder" title="Relational disorder">marital conflict</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>, or contribute to <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>. Alcoholism can also lead to <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">child neglect</a>, with subsequent lasting damage to the emotional development of children of people with alcohol use disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tcemh2006_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-tcemh2006-63">[63]</a></sup> For this reason, children of people with alcohol use disorders can develop a number of emotional problems. For example, they can become afraid of their parents, because of their unstable mood behaviors. They may develop shame over their inadequacy to liberate their parents from alcoholism and, as a result of this, may develop self-image problems, which can lead to depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_withdrawal">Alcohol withdrawal</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Alcohol withdrawal syndrome">Alcohol withdrawal syndrome</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kindling_(sedative-hypnotic_withdrawal)" title="Kindling (sedative-hypnotic withdrawal)">Kindling (sedative-hypnotic withdrawal)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children,_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2235" data-file-width="3142" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg/220px-A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg/330px-A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg/440px-A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children%2C_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_maniacal_man_is_visited_in_prison_by_his_children,_all_rui_Wellcome_V0019408.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Bottle_(etchings)" title="The Bottle (etchings)">"The bottle has done its work"</a>. Reproduction of an etching by G. Cruikshank, 1847.</div></div></div>
<p>As with similar substances with a sedative-hypnotic mechanism, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbiturates" title="Barbiturates">barbiturates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">benzodiazepines</a>, withdrawal from alcohol dependence can be fatal if it is not properly managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Alcohol's primary effect is the increase in stimulation of the <a href="/wiki/GABAA_receptor" title="GABAA receptor">GABA<sub>A</sub> receptor</a>, promoting <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> depression. With repeated heavy consumption of alcohol, these receptors are desensitized and reduced in number, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">tolerance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a>. When alcohol consumption is stopped too abruptly, the person's nervous system experiences uncontrolled <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapse</a> firing. This can result in symptoms that include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_(mood)" title="Anxiety (mood)">anxiety</a>, life-threatening <a href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a>, hallucinations, shakes and possible <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medical_toxicology_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_toxicology-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> systems are also involved, especially <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMDA" title="NMDA">NMDA</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman-1996_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-1996-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe acute withdrawal symptoms such as <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a> and seizures rarely occur after 1-week post cessation of alcohol. The acute withdrawal phase can be defined as lasting between one and three weeks. In the period of 3–6 weeks following cessation, anxiety, depression, fatigue, and sleep disturbance are common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20148778_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20148778-69">[69]</a></sup> Similar post-acute withdrawal symptoms have also been observed in animal models of alcohol dependence and withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kindling_(sedative-hypnotic_withdrawal)" title="Kindling (sedative-hypnotic withdrawal)">kindling effect</a> also occurs in people with alcohol use disorders whereby each subsequent withdrawal syndrome is more severe than the previous withdrawal episode; this is due to neuroadaptations which occur as a result of periods of abstinence followed by re-exposure to alcohol. Individuals who have had multiple withdrawal episodes are more likely to develop seizures and experience more severe anxiety during withdrawal from alcohol than alcohol-dependent individuals without a history of past alcohol withdrawal episodes. The kindling effect leads to persistent functional changes in brain neural circuits as well as to <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breese-2011_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breese-2011-71">[71]</a></sup> Kindling also results in the intensification of psychological symptoms of alcohol withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20148778_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20148778-69">[69]</a></sup> There are decision tools and questionnaires that help guide physicians in evaluating alcohol withdrawal. For example, the CIWA-Ar objectifies alcohol withdrawal symptoms in order to guide therapy decisions which allows for an efficient interview while at the same time retaining clinical usefulness, validity, and reliability, ensuring proper care for withdrawal patients, who can be in danger of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg/440px-Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg/660px-Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg/880px-Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mental_health_as_a_risk_factor_for_alcohol_dependency_or_abuse,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mental health as a risk factor for alcohol dependence or abuse.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4638" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg/220px-William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg/330px-William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg/440px-William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Hogarth_-_Gin_Lane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth's</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gin_Lane" title="Gin Lane">Gin Lane</a></i>, 1751</div></div></div>
<p>A complex combination of genetic and environmental factors influences the risk of the development of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch-2006_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch-2006-73">[73]</a></sup> Genes that influence the metabolism of alcohol also influence the risk of alcoholism, as can a family history of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup> There is compelling evidence that alcohol use at an early age may influence the <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression of genes</a> which increase the risk of alcohol dependence. These genetic and <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> results are regarded as consistent with large longitudinal population studies finding that the younger the age of drinking onset, the greater the prevalence of lifetime alcohol dependence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27677720_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27677720-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">Severe childhood trauma</a> is also associated with a general increase in the risk of drug dependency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch-2006_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch-2006-73">[73]</a></sup> Lack of peer and family support is associated with an increased risk of alcoholism developing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch-2006_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch-2006-73">[73]</a></sup> Genetics and adolescence are associated with an increased sensitivity to the neurotoxic effects of chronic alcohol misuse. <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">Cortical</a> degeneration due to the neurotoxic effects increases impulsive behaviour, which may contribute to the development, persistence and severity of alcohol use disorders. There is evidence that with abstinence, there is a reversal of at least some of the alcohol induced central nervous system damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crews-2009_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crews-2009-77">[77]</a></sup> The use of cannabis was associated with later problems with alcohol use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID26875671_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID26875671-78">[78]</a></sup> Alcohol use was associated with an increased probability of later use of tobacco and illegal drugs such as cannabis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID22712674_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID22712674-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Availability">Availability</span></h3>
<p>Alcohol is the most available, widely consumed, and widely misused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drug</a>. <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">Beer</a> alone is the world's most widely consumed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverage</a>; it is the third-most popular drink overall, after <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson_2005-81">[81]</a></sup> It is thought by some to be the oldest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermented_beverage" title="Fermented beverage">fermented beverage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_difference">Gender difference</span></h3>
<p>Based on combined data in the US from <a href="/wiki/Substance_Abuse_and_Mental_Health_Services_Administration" title="Substance Abuse and Mental Health Services Administration">SAMHSA</a>'s 2004–2005 National Surveys on Drug Use &amp; Health, the rate of past-year alcohol dependence or misuse among persons aged 12 or older varied by level of alcohol use: 44.7% of past month heavy drinkers, 18.5% binge drinkers, 3.8% past month non-binge drinkers, and 1.3% of those who did not drink alcohol in the past month met the criteria for alcohol dependence or misuse in the past year. Males had higher rates than females for all measures of drinking in the past month: any alcohol use (57.5% vs. 45%), binge drinking (30.8% vs. 15.1%), and heavy alcohol use (10.5% vs. 3.3%), and males were twice as likely as females to have met the criteria for alcohol dependence or misuse in the past year (10.5% vs. 5.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_variation">Genetic variation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Addiction#Genetic_factors" title="Addiction">Addiction § Genetic factors</a></div>
<p>There are genetic variations that affect the risk for alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch-2006_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch-2006-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-88">[88]</a></sup> Some of these variations are more common in individuals with ancestry from certain areas, for example Africa, East Asia, the Middle East and Europe. The variants with strongest effect are in genes that encode the main enzymes of alcohol metabolism, <i>ADH1B</i> and <i>ALDH2</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-88">[88]</a></sup> These genetic factors influence the rate at which alcohol and its initial metabolic product, acetaldehyde, are metabolized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup> They are found at different frequencies in people from different parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eng-2007_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eng-2007-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehlers-2007_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehlers-2007-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> allele <i>ADH1B*2</i> causes a more rapid metabolism of alcohol to acetaldehyde, and reduces risk for alcoholism;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup> it is most common in individuals from East Asia and the Middle East. The <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> allele <i>ADH1B*3</i> also causes a more rapid metabolism of alcohol. The allele ADH1B*3 is only found in some individuals of African descent and certain Native American tribes. African Americans and Native Americans with this allele have a reduced risk of developing alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehlers-2007_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehlers-2007-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott-2007_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-2007-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>, however, have a significantly higher rate of alcoholism than average; risk factors such as cultural environmental effects (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">trauma</a>) have been proposed to explain the higher rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szlemko-2006_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szlemko-2006-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spillane-2007_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spillane-2007-93">[93]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Aldehyde dehydrogenase">aldehyde dehydrogenase</a> allele <i>ALDH2*2</i> greatly reduces the rate at which acetaldehyde, the initial product of alcohol metabolism, is removed by conversion to acetate; it greatly reduces the risk for alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edenberg_2281–2297_74-7"><a href="#cite_note-Edenberg_2281–2297-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eng-2007_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eng-2007-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Genome-wide_association_study" title="Genome-wide association study">genome-wide association study</a> (GWAS) of more than 100,000 human individuals identified variants of the gene <i>KLB</i>, which encodes the <a href="/wiki/Transmembrane_protein" title="Transmembrane protein">transmembrane protein</a> β-<a href="/wiki/Klotho_(biology)" title="Klotho (biology)">Klotho</a>, as highly associated with alcohol consumption. The protein β-Klotho is an essential element in <a href="/wiki/Cell_surface_receptor" title="Cell surface receptor">cell surface receptors</a> for <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> involved in modulation of appetites for <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">simple sugars</a> and alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Several large GWAS have found differences in the genetics of alcohol consumption and alcohol dependence, although the two are to some degree related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_damage">DNA damage</span></h3>
<p>Alcohol-induced <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a>, when not properly repaired, may have a key role in the neurotoxicity induced by alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruman2012_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kruman2012-96">[96]</a></sup> Metabolic conversion of ethanol to <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a> can occur in the brain and the neurotoxic effects of ethanol appear to be associated with acetaldehyde induced DNA damages including DNA adducts and crosslinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruman2012_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kruman2012-96">[96]</a></sup> In addition to acetaldehyde, alcohol metabolism produces potentially genotoxic <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a>, which have been demonstrated to cause oxidative DNA damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruman2012_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kruman2012-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emil_Mayer_024.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="822" data-file-width="679" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Emil_Mayer_024.jpg/220px-Emil_Mayer_024.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Emil_Mayer_024.jpg/330px-Emil_Mayer_024.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Emil_Mayer_024.jpg/440px-Emil_Mayer_024.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emil_Mayer_024.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man drinking from a bottle of liquor while sitting on a boardwalk, ca. 1905–1914. Picture by Austrian photographer <a href="/wiki/Emil_Mayer" title="Emil Mayer">Emil Mayer</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Misuse, problem use, abuse, and heavy use of alcohol refer to improper use of alcohol, which may cause physical, social, or moral harm to the drinker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_american_heritage_dictionary_of_the_english_language_a12_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_american_heritage_dictionary_of_the_english_language_a12-97">[97]</a></sup> <i>The Dietary Guidelines for Americans</i> defines "moderate use" as no more than two alcoholic beverages a day for men and no more than one alcoholic beverage a day for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_on_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Alcoholism" title="National Institute on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism">National Institute on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism</a> (NIAAA) defines <a href="/wiki/Binge_drinking" title="Binge drinking">binge drinking</a> as the amount of alcohol leading to a blood alcohol content (BAC) of 0.08, which, for most adults, would be reached by consuming five drinks for men or four for women over a two-hour period. According to the NIAAA, men may be at risk for alcohol-related problems if their alcohol consumption exceeds 14 <a href="/wiki/Standard_drink" title="Standard drink">standard drinks</a> per week or 4 drinks per day, and women may be at risk if they have more than 7 standard drinks per week or 3 drinks per day. It defines a standard drink as one 12-ounce bottle of beer, one 5-ounce glass of wine, or 1.5 ounces of distilled spirits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Despite this risk, a 2014 report in the National Survey on Drug Use and Health found that only 10% of either "heavy drinkers" or "binge drinkers" defined according to the above criteria also met the criteria for alcohol dependence, while only 1.3% of non-binge drinkers met the criteria. An inference drawn from this study is that evidence-based policy strategies and clinical preventive services may effectively reduce binge drinking without requiring addiction treatment in most cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esser2014_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esser2014-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcoholism">Alcoholism</span></h4>
<p>The term <i>alcoholism</i> is commonly used amongst laypeople, but the word is poorly defined. Despite the imprecision inherent in the term, there have been attempts to define how the word <i>alcoholism</i> should be interpreted when encountered. In 1992, it was defined by the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_on_Alcoholism_and_Drug_Dependence" title="National Council on Alcoholism and Drug Dependence">National Council on Alcoholism and Drug Dependence</a> (NCADD) and <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Addiction_Medicine" title="American Society of Addiction Medicine">ASAM</a> as "a primary, chronic disease characterized by impaired control over drinking, preoccupation with the drug alcohol, use of alcohol despite adverse consequences, and distortions in thinking."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MeSH" title="MeSH">MeSH</a> has had an entry for <i>alcoholism</i> since 1999, and references the 1992 definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The WHO calls <i>alcoholism</i> "a term of long-standing use and variable meaning", and use of the term was disfavored by a 1979 WHO expert committee.
</p><p>In professional and research contexts, the term <i>alcoholism</i> is not currently favored, but rather <i>alcohol abuse</i>, <i>alcohol dependence</i>, or <i>alcohol use disorder</i> are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-17"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2013-2">[2]</a></sup> Talbot (1989) observes that alcoholism in the classical disease model follows a progressive course: if a person continues to drink, their condition will worsen. This will lead to harmful consequences in their life, physically, mentally, emotionally and socially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomb1999_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomb1999-103">[103]</a></sup> Johnson (1980) explores the emotional progression of the addict's response to alcohol. He looks at this in four phases. The first two are considered "normal" drinking and the last two are viewed as "typical" alcoholic drinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomb1999_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomb1999-103">[103]</a></sup> Johnson's four phases consist of:
</p>
<ol><li>Learning the mood swing. A person is introduced to alcohol (in some cultures this can happen at a relatively young age), and the person enjoys the happy feeling it produces. At this stage, there is no emotional cost.</li>
<li>Seeking the mood swing. A person will drink to regain that feeling of euphoria experienced in phase 1; the drinking will increase as more intoxication is required to achieve the same effect. Again at this stage, there are no significant consequences.</li>
<li>At the third stage there are physical and social consequences, i.e., hangovers, family problems, work problems, etc. A person will continue to drink excessively, disregarding the problems.</li>
<li>The fourth stage can be detrimental, as Johnson cites it as a risk for premature death. As a person now drinks to feel normal, they block out the feelings of overwhelming guilt, remorse, anxiety, and shame they experience when sober.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomb1999_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomb1999-103">[103]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DSM_and_ICD">DSM and ICD</span></h4>
<p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a> (DSM) is the most common diagnostic guide for substance use disorders, whereas most countries use the <a href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_Diseases" title="International Classification of Diseases">International Classification of Diseases</a> (ICD) for diagnostic (and other) purposes. The two manuals use similar but not identical nomenclature to classify alcohol problems.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Manual
</th>
<th>Nomenclature
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">DSM-IV</a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Alcohol_abuse" title="Alcohol abuse">Alcohol abuse</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dependence" title="Alcohol dependence">Alcohol dependence</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Alcohol abuse - repeated use despite recurrent adverse consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_apa_dictionary_of_psychology_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_apa_dictionary_of_psychology-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>Alcohol dependence - <i>alcohol abuse</i> combined with <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">withdrawal</a>, and an uncontrollable drive to drink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_apa_dictionary_of_psychology_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-the_apa_dictionary_of_psychology-104">[104]</a></sup> The term "alcoholism" was split into "alcohol abuse" and "alcohol dependence" in 1980's DSM-III, and in 1987's DSM-III-R behavioral symptoms were moved from "abuse" to "dependence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlDiagnostic_Criteria_for_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Dependence_–_Alcohol_Alert_No._30-1995_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlDiagnostic_Criteria_for_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Dependence_–_Alcohol_Alert_No._30-1995-105">[105]</a></sup> Some scholars suggested that <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a> merges alcohol abuse and alcohol dependence into a single new entry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18729609_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18729609-106">[106]</a></sup> named "alcohol-use disorder".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlProposed_Revision_|_APA_DSM-5_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlProposed_Revision_|_APA_DSM-5-107">[107]</a></sup></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a>
</td>
<td>Alcohol use disorder
</td>
<td>"A problematic pattern of alcohol use leading to clinically significant impairment or distress, as manifested by [two or more symptoms out of a total of 12], occurring within a 12-month period ...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">ICD-10</a>
</td>
<td>Alcohol harmful use, or Alcohol dependence syndrome
</td>
<td>Definitions are similar to that of the DSM-IV. The World Health Organization uses the term "alcohol dependence syndrome" rather than alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ladtpwho_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ladtpwho-29">[29]</a></sup> The concept of "harmful use" (as opposed to "abuse") was introduced in 1992's ICD-10 to minimize underreporting of damage in the absence of dependence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlDiagnostic_Criteria_for_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Dependence_–_Alcohol_Alert_No._30-1995_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-urlDiagnostic_Criteria_for_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Dependence_–_Alcohol_Alert_No._30-1995-105">[105]</a></sup> The term "alcoholism" was removed from ICD between ICD-8/ICDA-8 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICD-9" title="ICD-9">ICD-9</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlA_System_to_Convert_ICD_Diagnostic_Codes_for_Alcohol_Research_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlA_System_to_Convert_ICD_Diagnostic_Codes_for_Alcohol_Research-109">[109]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/ICD-11" title="ICD-11">ICD-11</a>
</td>
<td>Episode of harmful use of alcohol, Harmful pattern of use of alcohol, or Alcohol dependence
</td>
<td>
<ul><li><i>Episode of harmful use of alcohol</i> - "A single episode of use of alcohol that has caused damage to a person's physical or mental health or has resulted in behaviour leading to harm to the health of others ...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Harmful pattern of use of alcohol</i> - "A pattern of alcohol use that has caused damage to a person's physical or mental health or has resulted in behaviour leading to harm to the health of others ...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Alcohol dependence</i> - "Alcohol dependence is a disorder of regulation of alcohol use arising from repeated or continuous use of alcohol. The characteristic feature is a strong internal drive to use alcohol ... The features of dependence are usually evident over a period of at least 12 months but the diagnosis may be made if alcohol use is continuous (daily or almost daily) for at least 1 month."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></li></ul>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_barriers">Social barriers</span></h3>
<p>Attitudes and social stereotypes can create barriers to the detection and treatment of alcohol use disorder. This is more of a barrier for women than men.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The reason for this is unclear. (May 2022)">why?</span></a></i>]</sup> Fear of stigmatization may lead women to deny that they have a medical condition, to hide their drinking, and to drink alone. This pattern, in turn, leads family, physicians, and others to be less likely to suspect that a woman they know has alcohol use disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blume_Laura_N.,_Nielson_Nancy_H.,_Riggs_Joseph_A.,_et_all_1998_861–870-39">[39]</a></sup> In contrast, reduced fear of stigma may lead men to admit that they are having a medical condition, to display their drinking publicly, and to drink in groups. This pattern, in turn, leads family, physicians, and others to be more likely to suspect that a man they know is someone with an alcohol use disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karrol_2002_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Karrol_2002-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Screening">Screening</span></h3>
<p>Screening is recommended among those over the age of 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Several tools may be used to detect a loss of control of alcohol use. These tools are mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_report_study" title="Self report study">self-reports</a> in questionnaire form. Another common theme is a score or tally that sums up the general severity of alcohol use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kahan-1996_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kahan-1996-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/CAGE_questionnaire" title="CAGE questionnaire">CAGE questionnaire</a>, named for its four questions, is one such example that may be used to screen patients quickly in a doctor's office.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Two "yes" responses indicate that the respondent should be investigated further.
</p><p>The questionnaire asks the following questions:
</p>
<ol><li>Have you ever felt you needed to <b>C</b>ut down on your drinking?</li>
<li>Have people <b>A</b>nnoyed you by criticizing your drinking?</li>
<li>Have you ever felt <b>G</b>uilty about drinking?</li>
<li>Have you ever felt you needed a drink first thing in the morning (<b>E</b>ye-opener) to steady your nerves or to get rid of a hangover?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li></ol></blockquote>
<dl><dd>The CAGE questionnaire has demonstrated a high effectiveness in detecting alcohol-related problems; however, it has limitations in people with less severe alcohol-related problems, white women and college students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Other tests are sometimes used for the detection of alcohol dependence, such as the <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_Dependence_Data_Questionnaire" title="Alcohol Dependence Data Questionnaire">Alcohol Dependence Data Questionnaire</a>, which is a more sensitive diagnostic test than the <a href="/wiki/CAGE_questionnaire" title="CAGE questionnaire">CAGE questionnaire</a>. It helps distinguish a diagnosis of alcohol dependence from one of heavy alcohol use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michigan_Alcohol_Screening_Test" title="Michigan Alcohol Screening Test">Michigan Alcohol Screening Test</a> (MAST) is a screening tool for alcoholism widely used by courts to determine the appropriate sentencing for people convicted of alcohol-related offenses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Driving_under_the_influence" title="Driving under the influence">driving under the influence</a> being the most common. The <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_Use_Disorders_Identification_Test" title="Alcohol Use Disorders Identification Test">Alcohol Use Disorders Identification Test</a> (AUDIT), a screening questionnaire developed by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, is unique in that it has been validated in six countries and is used internationally. Like the CAGE questionnaire, it uses a simple set of questions – a high score earning a deeper investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paddington_Alcohol_Test" title="Paddington Alcohol Test">Paddington Alcohol Test</a> (PAT) was designed to screen for alcohol-related problems amongst those attending <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accident_and_Emergency_department" title="Accident and Emergency department">Accident and Emergency departments</a>. It concords well with the AUDIT questionnaire but is administered in a fifth of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urine_and_blood_tests">Urine and blood tests</span></h3>
<p>There are reliable tests for the actual use of alcohol, one common test being that of <a href="/wiki/Blood_alcohol_content" title="Blood alcohol content">blood alcohol content</a> (BAC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> These tests do not differentiate people with alcohol use disorders from people without; however, long-term heavy drinking does have a few recognizable effects on the body, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Macrocytosis" title="Macrocytosis">Macrocytosis</a> (enlarged <a href="/wiki/Mean_corpuscular_volume" title="Mean corpuscular volume">MCV</a>)</li>
<li>Elevated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_glutamyl_transpeptidase" title="Gamma glutamyl transpeptidase">GGT</a></li>
<li>Moderate elevation of <a href="/wiki/Aspartate_transaminase" title="Aspartate transaminase">AST</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alanine_transaminase" title="Alanine transaminase">ALT</a> and an AST: ALT ratio of 2:1</li>
<li>High <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate_deficient_transferrin" title="Carbohydrate deficient transferrin">carbohydrate deficient transferrin</a> (CDT)</li></ul>
<p>With regard to alcoholism, BAC is useful to judge <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_tolerance" title="Alcohol tolerance">alcohol tolerance</a>, which in turn is a sign of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM5_4-18"><a href="#cite_note-DSM5-4">[4]</a></sup> Electrolyte and acid-base abnormalities including <a href="/wiki/Hypokalemia" title="Hypokalemia">hypokalemia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypomagnesemia" title="Hypomagnesemia">hypomagnesemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyponatremia" title="Hyponatremia">hyponatremia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperuricemia" title="Hyperuricemia">hyperuricemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_alkalosis" title="Respiratory alkalosis">respiratory alkalosis</a> are common in people with alcohol use disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIHHx_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIHHx-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, none of these blood tests for biological markers is as sensitive as screening questionnaires.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_education" title="Alcohol education">Alcohol education</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and other regional bodies, national governments and parliaments have formed alcohol policies in order to reduce the harm of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alchwho2010_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-alchwho2010-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apitwhoer_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-apitwhoer-125">[125]</a></sup> Increasing the age at which licit drugs that are susceptible to misuse, such as alcohol, can be purchased, and banning or restricting alcohol beverage advertising are common methods to reduce alcohol use among adolescents and young adults in particular. Credible, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evidence_based" title="Evidence based">evidence-based</a> educational campaigns in the mass media about the consequences of alcohol misuse have been recommended. Guidelines for parents to prevent alcohol misuse amongst adolescents, and for helping young people with mental health problems have also been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acdacpovfa_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-acdacpovfa-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<p>Treatments are varied because there are multiple perspectives of alcoholism. Those who approach alcoholism as a medical condition or disease recommend differing treatments from, for instance, those who approach the condition as one of social choice. Most treatments focus on helping people discontinue their alcohol intake, followed up with life training and/or social support to help them resist a return to alcohol use. Since alcoholism involves multiple factors which encourage a person to continue drinking, they must all be addressed to successfully prevent a relapse. An example of this kind of treatment is detoxification followed by a combination of supportive therapy, attendance at self-help groups, and ongoing development of coping mechanisms. Much of the treatment community for alcoholism supports an abstinence-based <a href="/wiki/Zero_tolerance" title="Zero tolerance">zero tolerance</a> approach popularized by the 12 step program of <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a>; however, some prefer a <a href="/wiki/Harm_reduction" title="Harm reduction">harm-reduction</a> approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbard_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbard-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cessation_of_alcohol_intake">Cessation of alcohol intake</span></h3>
