<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_detoxification" title="Alcohol detoxification">Alcohol detoxification</a></div>
<p>Medical treatment for <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_detoxification" title="Alcohol detoxification">alcohol detoxification</a> usually involves administration of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">benzodiazepine</a>, in order to ameliorate <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Alcohol withdrawal syndrome">alcohol withdrawal syndrome</a>'s adverse impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The addition of <a href="/wiki/Phenobarbital" title="Phenobarbital">phenobarbital</a> improves outcomes if benzodiazepine administration lacks the usually efficacy, and phenobarbital alone might be an effective treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Propofol" title="Propofol">Propofol</a> also might enhance treatment for individuals showing limited therapeutic response to a benzodiazepine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Individuals who are only at risk of mild to moderate withdrawal symptoms can be treated as outpatients. Individuals at risk of a severe withdrawal syndrome as well as those who have significant or acute comorbid conditions can be treated as inpatients. Direct treatment can be followed by a treatment program for alcohol dependence or alcohol use disorder to attempt to reduce the risk of relapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blondell-2005_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Blondell-2005-9">[9]</a></sup> Experiences following alcohol withdrawal, such as depressed mood and anxiety, can take weeks or months to abate while other symptoms persist longer due to persisting neuroadaptations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20148778_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20148778-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological">Psychological</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/330px-Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/440px-Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alcoholics_Anonymous_Regional_Service_Center_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A regional service center for <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Various forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_therapy" title="Group therapy">group therapy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a> are often utilized to encourage and support abstinence from alcohol, or to reduce alcohol consumption to levels that are not associated with adverse outcomes. Mutual-aid group-counseling is an approach used to facilitate relapse prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan-Lopez-2006_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-Lopez-2006-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a> was one of the earliest organizations formed to provide mutual <a href="/wiki/Peer_support" title="Peer support">peer support</a> and it is still the largest. Others include <a href="/wiki/LifeRing_Secular_Recovery" title="LifeRing Secular Recovery">LifeRing Secular Recovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/SMART_Recovery" title="SMART Recovery">SMART Recovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_for_Sobriety" title="Women for Sobriety">Women for Sobriety</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Secular_Organizations_for_Sobriety" title="Secular Organizations for Sobriety">Secular Organizations for Sobriety</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Manualized<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Twelve Step Facilitation (TSF) interventions (i.e. therapy which encourages active, long-term Alcoholics Anonymous participation) for Alcohol Use Disorder lead to higher abstinence rates, compared to other clinical interventions and to wait-list control groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coch2020_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-coch2020-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moderate_drinking">Moderate drinking</span></h3>
<p>Rationing and moderation programs such as <a href="/wiki/Moderation_Management" title="Moderation Management">Moderation Management</a> and DrinkWise do not mandate complete abstinence. While most people with alcohol use disorders are unable to limit their drinking in this way, some return to moderate drinking. A 2002 US study by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_on_Alcohol_Abuse_and_Alcoholism" title="National Institute on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism">National Institute on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism</a> (NIAAA) showed that 17.7% of individuals diagnosed as alcohol dependent more than one year prior returned to low-risk drinking. This group, however, showed fewer initial symptoms of dependency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAWSON2005_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAWSON2005-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>A follow-up study, using the same subjects that were judged to be in remission in 2001–2002, examined the rates of return to problem drinking in 2004–2005. The study found abstinence from alcohol was the most stable form of remission for recovering alcoholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAWSON2007_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAWSON2007-137">[137]</a></sup> There was also a 1973 study showing chronic alcoholics drinking moderately again,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sobell1973_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sobell1973-138">[138]</a></sup> but a 1982 follow-up showed that 95% of subjects were not able to maintain drinking in moderation over the long term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pendery1982_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pendery1982-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pendery1982NYT_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pendery1982NYT-140">[140]</a></sup> Another study was a long-term (60 year) follow-up of two groups of alcoholic men which concluded that "return to controlled drinking rarely persisted for much more than a decade without relapse or evolution into abstinence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VAILLANT2003_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-VAILLANT2003-141">[141]</a></sup> Internet based measures appear to be useful at least in the short term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span></h3>
<p>In the United States there are four approved medications for alcoholism: acamprosate, two methods of using naltrexone and disulfiram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Acamprosate" title="Acamprosate">Acamprosate</a> may stabilise the brain chemistry that is altered due to alcohol dependence via antagonising the actions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> which is hyperactive in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-withdrawal" title="Post-withdrawal">post-withdrawal</a> phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> By reducing excessive NMDA activity which occurs at the onset of alcohol withdrawal, acamprosate can reduce or prevent alcohol withdrawal related neurotoxicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20201812_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20201812-145">[145]</a></sup> Acamprosate reduces the risk of relapse amongst alcohol-dependent persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA2014_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA2014-147">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naltrexone" title="Naltrexone">Naltrexone</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitive_antagonist" title="Competitive antagonist">competitive antagonist</a> for opioid receptors, effectively blocking the effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endorphin" title="Endorphin">endorphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioids</a>. Naltrexone is used to decrease cravings for alcohol and encourage abstinence. Alcohol causes the body to release endorphins, which in turn release dopamine and activate the reward pathways; hence in the body Naltrexone reduces the pleasurable effects from consuming alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Evidence supports a reduced risk of relapse among alcohol-dependent persons and a decrease in excessive drinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA2014_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA2014-147">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nalmefene" title="Nalmefene">Nalmefene</a> also appears effective and works in a similar manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA2014_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA2014-147">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">Disulfiram</a> prevents the elimination of <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a>, a chemical the body produces when breaking down ethanol. Acetaldehyde itself is the cause of many <a href="/wiki/Hangover" title="Hangover">hangover</a> symptoms from alcohol use. The overall effect is discomfort when alcohol is ingested: an extremely fast-acting and long-lasting, uncomfortable hangover.</li></ul>
<p>Several other drugs are also used and many are under investigation.
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">Benzodiazepines</a>, while useful in the management of acute alcohol withdrawal, if used long-term can cause a worse outcome in alcoholism. Alcoholics on chronic benzodiazepines have a lower rate of achieving abstinence from alcohol than those not taking benzodiazepines. This class of drugs is commonly prescribed to alcoholics for insomnia or anxiety management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Initiating prescriptions of benzodiazepines or sedative-hypnotics in individuals in recovery has a high rate of relapse with one author reporting more than a quarter of people relapsed after being prescribed sedative-hypnotics. Those who are long-term users of benzodiazepines should not be withdrawn rapidly, as severe anxiety and panic may develop, which are known risk factors for alcohol use disorder relapse. Taper regimes of 6–12 months have been found to be the most successful, with reduced intensity of withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbimide" title="Calcium carbimide">Calcium carbimide</a> works in the same way as disulfiram; it has an advantage in that the occasional adverse effects of disulfiram, <a href="/wiki/Hepatotoxicity" title="Hepatotoxicity">hepatotoxicity</a> and drowsiness, do not occur with calcium carbimide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ondansetron" title="Ondansetron">Ondansetron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topiramate" title="Topiramate">topiramate</a> are supported by tentative evidence in people with certain genetic patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soy2017_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soy2017-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cs2017_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cs2017-154">[154]</a></sup> Evidence for ondansetron is stronger in people who have recently started to abuse alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soy2017_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soy2017-153">[153]</a></sup> Topiramate is a derivative of the naturally occurring sugar monosaccharide D-fructose. Review articles characterize topiramate as showing "encouraging",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soy2017_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Soy2017-153">[153]</a></sup> "promising",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soy2017_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-Soy2017-153">[153]</a></sup> "efficacious",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and "insufficient"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> results in the treatment of alcohol use disorders.</li></ul>
<p>Evidence does not support the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitors" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a> (SSRIs), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tricyclic_antidepressants" title="Tricyclic antidepressants">tricyclic antidepressants</a> (TCAs), <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">antipsychotics</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gabapentin" title="Gabapentin">gabapentin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA2014_147-3"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA2014-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Topiramate" title="Topiramate">Topiramate</a>, a derivative of the naturally occurring sugar monosaccharide D-fructose, has been found effective in helping alcoholics quit or cut back on the amount they drink. Evidence suggests that topiramate antagonizes excitatory glutamate receptors, inhibits dopamine release, and enhances inhibitory gamma-aminobutyric acid function. A 2008 review of the effectiveness of topiramate concluded that the results of published trials are promising, however as of 2008, data was insufficient to support using topiramate in conjunction with brief weekly compliance counseling as a first-line agent for alcohol dependence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> A 2010 review found that topiramate may be superior to existing alcohol pharmacotherapeutic options. Topiramate effectively reduces craving and alcohol withdrawal severity as well as improving quality-of-life-ratings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Baclofen" title="Baclofen">Baclofen</a>, a <a href="/wiki/GABAB_receptor" title="GABAB receptor">GABAB receptor</a> agonist, is under study for the treatment of alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> According to a 2017 Cochrane Systematic Review, there is insufficient evidence to determine the effectiveness or safety for the use of baclofen for withdrawal symptoms in alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin" title="Psilocybin">Psilocybin</a>-assisted psychotherapy is under study for the treatment of patients with alcohol use disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual_addictions_and_dependencies">Dual addictions and dependencies</span></h3>
<p>Alcoholics may also require treatment for other psychotropic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" title="Drug addiction">drug addictions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">drug dependencies</a>. The most common dual dependence syndrome with alcohol dependence is <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine_dependence" title="Benzodiazepine dependence">benzodiazepine dependence</a>, with studies showing 10–20% of alcohol-dependent individuals had problems of dependence and/or misuse problems of benzodiazepine drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Diazepam" title="Diazepam">diazepam</a> or <a href="/wiki/Clonazepam" title="Clonazepam">clonazepam</a>. These drugs are, like alcohol, <a href="/wiki/Depressant" title="Depressant">depressants</a>. Benzodiazepines may be used legally, if they are prescribed by doctors for anxiety problems or other mood disorders, or they may be purchased as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_drugs" title="Illegal drugs">illegal drugs</a>. Benzodiazepine use increases cravings for alcohol and the volume of alcohol consumed by problem drinkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Benzodiazepine dependency requires careful reduction in dosage to avoid <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Benzodiazepine withdrawal syndrome">benzodiazepine withdrawal syndrome</a> and other health consequences. Dependence on other sedative-hypnotics such as <a href="/wiki/Zolpidem" title="Zolpidem">zolpidem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zopiclone" title="Zopiclone">zopiclone</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Opiate" title="Opiate">opiates</a> and illegal drugs is common in alcoholics. Alcohol itself is a sedative-hypnotic and is cross-tolerant with other sedative-hypnotics such as <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturates</a>, benzodiazepines and <a href="/wiki/Nonbenzodiazepine" title="Nonbenzodiazepine">nonbenzodiazepines</a>. Dependence upon and withdrawal from sedative-hypnotics can be medically severe and, as with alcohol withdrawal, there is a risk of <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychosis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizures</a> if not properly managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dolpdaa_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-dolpdaa-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcohol_consumption_per_person,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg/330px-Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg/495px-Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg/660px-Alcohol_consumption_per_person%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alcohol_consumption_per_person,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alcohol consumption per person 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates that as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alcoholism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there are 380 million people with alcoholism worldwide (5.1% of the population over 15 years of age).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un2020_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-un2020-12">[12]</a></sup> Substance use disorders are a major <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> problem facing many countries. "The most common substance of abuse/dependence in patients presenting for treatment is alcohol."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbard_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbard-127">[127]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the number of 'dependent drinkers' was calculated as over 2.8 million in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosu_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosu-166">[166]</a></sup> About 12% of American adults have had an alcohol dependence problem at some time in their life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> In the United States and Western Europe, 10 to 20% of men and 5 to 10% of women at some point in their lives will meet criteria for alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> had the highest death rate from alcohol in Europe in 2015 at 8.8 per 100,000 population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> In the United States, 30% of people admitted to hospital have a problem related to alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the medical and scientific communities, there is a broad consensus regarding alcoholism as a disease state. For example, the American Medical Association considers alcohol a drug and states that "drug addiction is a chronic, relapsing brain disease characterized by compulsive drug seeking and use despite often devastating consequences. It results from a complex interplay of biological vulnerability, environmental exposure, and developmental factors (e.g., stage of brain maturity)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-171">[171]</a></sup> Alcoholism has a higher prevalence among men, though, in recent decades, the proportion of female alcoholics has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walter_H.,_Gutierrez_K.,_Ramskogler_K.,_Hertling_I.,_Dvorak_A.,_Lesch_O.M.,_et_al._2003_253–268_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_H.,_Gutierrez_K.,_Ramskogler_K.,_Hertling_I.,_Dvorak_A.,_Lesch_O.M.,_et_al._2003_253–268-40">[40]</a></sup> Current evidence indicates that in both men and women, alcoholism is 50–60% genetically determined, leaving 40–50% for environmental influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Most alcoholics develop alcoholism during adolescence or young adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch-2006_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch-2006-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/290px-Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/435px-Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/580px-Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alcohol_use_disorders_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alcohol use disorders deaths per million persons in 2012 <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1054258005" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="column-count: 3;"> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff20; color:black;"> </span> 0–0</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 1–3</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 4–6</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 7–13</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 14–20</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 21–37</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 38–52</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 53–255</div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>Alcoholism often reduces a person's life expectancy by around ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schu2014_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schu2014-27">[27]</a></sup> The most common cause of death in alcoholics is from cardiovascular complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuskin-2006_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuskin-2006-173">[173]</a></sup> There is a high rate of <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> in chronic alcoholics, which increases the longer a person drinks. Approximately 3–15% of alcoholics commit suicide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> and research has found that over 50% of all suicides are associated with alcohol or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_dependence" title="Drug dependence">drug dependence</a>. This is believed to be due to alcohol causing physiological distortion of brain chemistry, as well as social isolation. Suicide is also very common in adolescent alcohol abusers, with 25% of suicides in adolescents being related to alcohol abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-understanding_suicidal_behaviour_a02_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-understanding_suicidal_behaviour_a02-175">[175]</a></sup> Among those with <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dependence" title="Alcohol dependence">alcohol dependence</a> after one year, some met the criteria for low-risk drinking, even though only 25.5% of the group received any treatment, with the breakdown as follows: 25% were found to be still dependent, 27.3% were in partial remission (some symptoms persist), 11.8% asymptomatic drinkers (consumption increases chances of relapse) and 35.9% were fully recovered – made up of 17.7% low-risk drinkers plus 18.2% abstainers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAAA2002_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIAAA2002-176">[176]</a></sup> In contrast, however, the results of a long-term (60-year) follow-up of two groups of alcoholic men indicated that "return to controlled drinking rarely persisted for much more than a decade without relapse or evolution into abstinence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VAILLANT2003_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-VAILLANT2003-141">[141]</a></sup> There was also "return-to-controlled drinking, as reported in short-term studies, is often a mirage."
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4632" data-file-width="6286" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg/220px-Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg/330px-Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg/440px-Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adriaen_Brouwer_-_Inn_with_drunken_peasants.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adriaen_Brouwer" title="Adriaen Brouwer">Adriaen Brouwer</a>, <i>Inn with Drunken Peasants</i>, 1620s</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1505" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="403" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg/150px-1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg/225px-1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg/300px-1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1904_Claim_of_Alcoholism_Being_Disease4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1904 advertisement describing alcoholism as a disease.</div></div></div>
<p>Historically the name <i><a href="/wiki/Dipsomania" title="Dipsomania">dipsomania</a></i> was coined by German physician <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Wilhelm_Hufeland" title="Christoph Wilhelm Hufeland">C. W. Hufeland</a> in 1819 before it was superseded by <i>alcoholism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lexikon_psychiatrie_psychotherapie_medizinische_psychologie_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-lexikon_psychiatrie_psychotherapie_medizinische_psychologie-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> That term now has a more specific meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The term <i>alcoholism</i> was first used in 1849 by the Swedish physician <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Huss" title="Magnus Huss">Magnus Huss</a> to describe the systemic adverse effects of alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alcohol has a long history of use and misuse throughout recorded history. Biblical, Egyptian and Babylonian sources record the history of abuse and dependence on alcohol. In some ancient cultures alcohol was worshiped and in others, its misuse was condemned. Excessive alcohol misuse and drunkenness were recognized as causing social problems even thousands of years ago. However, the defining of habitual drunkenness as it was then known as and its adverse consequences were not well established medically until the 18th century. In 1647 a Greek monk named Agapios was the first to document that chronic alcohol misuse was associated with toxicity to the nervous system and body which resulted in a range of medical disorders such as seizures, paralysis, and internal bleeding. In the 1910s and 1920s, the effects of alcohol misuse and chronic drunkenness boosted membership of the <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance movement</a> and led to the <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibition of alcohol</a> in many Western countries, nationwide bans on the production, importation, transportation, and sale of alcoholic beverages that generally remained in place until the late 1920s or early 1930s; these policies resulted in the decline of death rates from cirrhosis and alcoholism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blocker2006_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blocker2006-181">[181]</a></sup> In 2005, alcohol dependence and misuse was estimated to cost the US economy approximately 220 billion dollars per year, more than cancer and obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soa2008_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-soa2008-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_deaths_through_alcohol" title="List of deaths through alcohol">List of deaths through alcohol</a></div>
<p>The various health problems associated with long-term alcohol consumption are generally perceived as detrimental to society, for example, money due to lost labor-hours, medical costs due to injuries due to drunkenness and organ damage from long-term use, and secondary treatment costs, such as the costs of rehabilitation facilities and detoxification centers. Alcohol use is a major contributing factor for <a href="/wiki/Head_injury" title="Head injury">head injuries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whiplash_(medicine)" title="Whiplash (medicine)">motor vehicle injuries</a> (27%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpersonal_violence" title="Interpersonal violence">interpersonal violence</a> (18%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicides" title="Suicides">suicides</a> (18%), and <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> (13%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Beyond the financial costs that alcohol consumption imposes, there are also significant social costs to both the alcoholic and their family and friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jessica_Kingsley_Publishers-59">[59]</a></sup> For instance, alcohol consumption by a pregnant woman can lead to an incurable and damaging condition known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetal_alcohol_syndrome" title="Fetal alcohol syndrome">fetal alcohol syndrome</a>, which often results in <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_deficit" title="Cognitive deficit">cognitive deficits</a>, mental health problems, an inability to live independently and an increased risk of criminal behaviour, all of which can cause emotional stress for parents and caregivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Estimates of the economic costs of alcohol misuse, collected by the World Health Organization, vary from one to six% of a country's GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> One Australian estimate pegged alcohol's social costs at 24% of all drug misuse costs; a similar Canadian study concluded alcohol's share was 41%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> One study quantified the cost to the UK of <i>all</i> forms of alcohol misuse in 2001 as £18.5–20 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosu_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosu-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> All economic costs in the United States in 2006 have been estimated at $223.5 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Hitting_rock_bottom"></span>The idea of <b>hitting rock bottom</b> refers to an experience of <a href="/wiki/Psychological_stress" title="Psychological stress">stress</a> that can be attributed to alcohol misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> There is no single definition for this idea, and people may identify their own lowest points in terms of lost jobs, lost relationships, health problems, legal problems, or other consequences of alcohol misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-191">[191]</a></sup> The concept is promoted by 12-step recovery groups and researchers using the <a href="/wiki/Transtheoretical_model" title="Transtheoretical model">transtheoretical model</a> of motivation for behavior change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-191">[191]</a></sup> The first use of this slang phrase in the formal <a href="/wiki/Medical_literature" title="Medical literature">medical literature</a> appeared in a 1965 <a href="/wiki/Literature_review" title="Literature review">review</a> in the <i>British Medical Journal</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-191">[191]</a></sup> which said that some men refused treatment until they "hit rock bottom", but that treatment was generally more successful for "the alcohol addict who has friends and family to support him" than for impoverished and homeless addicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">Stereotypes</a> of alcoholics are often found in <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>. The "<a href="/wiki/Town_drunk" title="Town drunk">town drunk</a>" is a <a href="/wiki/Stock_character" title="Stock character">stock character</a> in Western popular culture. Stereotypes of drunkenness may be based on <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, as in the fictional depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> as heavy drinkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Studies by social psychologists Stivers and Greeley attempt to document the perceived prevalence of high alcohol consumption amongst the Irish in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Alcohol consumption is relatively similar between many European cultures, the United States, and Australia. In Asian countries that have a high gross domestic product, there is heightened drinking compared to other Asian countries, but it is nowhere near as high as it is in other countries like the United States. It is also inversely seen, with countries that have very low gross domestic product showing high alcohol consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> In a study done on Korean immigrants in Canada, they reported alcohol was even an integral part of their meal, and is the only time solo drinking should occur. They also believe alcohol is necessary at any social event as it helps conversations start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peyote" title="Peyote">Peyote</a>, a psychoactive agent, has even shown promise in treating alcoholism. Alcohol had actually replaced peyote as <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>' psychoactive agent of choice in rituals when peyote was outlawed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Pendery1982NYT-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Pendery1982NYT_140-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1982/06/28/us/alcholism-study-under-new-attack.html" rel="nofollow">"Alcoholism Study Under New Attack"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>. 28 June 1982. <q>The new article will say that, 10 years later, four of the 20 had died from alcohol-related causes. Another subject, who could not be found, was reported gravely disabled.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=Alcoholism+Study+Under+New+Attack&amp;rft.date=1982-06-28&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F1982%2F06%2F28%2Fus%2Falcholism-study-under-new-attack.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlcoholism"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPaniTroguPaciniMaremmani2014">Pani PP, Trogu E, Pacini M, Maremmani I (February 2014). "Anticonvulsants for alcohol dependence". <i>The Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews</i>. <b>2</b> (2): CD008544. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2F14651858.CD008544.pub2" rel="nofollow">10.1002/14651858.CD008544.pub2</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24523233" rel="nofollow">24523233</a>. <q>At the current stage of research, evidence supporting the clinical use of anticonvulsants to treat alcohol dependence is insufficient. Results are conditioned by heterogeneity and by the low number and quality of studies comparing anticonvulsants versus other medications. The uncertainty associated with these results leaves to clinicians the need to balance the possible benefits/risks of treatment with anticonvulsants versus other medications as supported by evidence of efficacy.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Cochrane+Database+of+Systematic+Reviews&amp;rft.atitle=Anticonvulsants+for+alcohol+dependence&amp;rft.volume=2&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=CD008544&amp;rft.date=2014-02&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2F14651858.CD008544.pub2&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24523233&amp;rft.aulast=Pani&amp;rft.aufirst=PP&amp;rft.au=Trogu%2C+E&amp;rft.au=Pacini%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Maremmani%2C+I&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlcoholism"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Blocker2006-181"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Blocker2006_181-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBlocker2006">Blocker JS (February 2006). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1470475" rel="nofollow">"Did prohibition really work? Alcohol prohibition as a public health innovation"</a>. <i>American Journal of Public Health</i>. <b>96</b> (2): 233–43. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2105%2FAJPH.2005.065409" rel="nofollow">10.2105/AJPH.2005.065409</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1470475" rel="nofollow">1470475</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16380559" rel="nofollow">16380559</a>. <q>Nevertheless, once Prohibition became the law of the land, many citizens decided to obey it. Referendum results in the immediate post-Volstead period showed widespread support, and the Supreme Court quickly fended off challenges to the new law. Death rates from cirrhosis and alcoholism, alcoholic psychosis hospital admissions, and drunkenness arrests all declined steeply during the latter years of the 1910s, when both the cultural and the legal climate were increasingly inhospitable to drink, and in the early years after National Prohibition went into effect.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Public+Health&amp;rft.atitle=Did+prohibition+really+work%3F+Alcohol+prohibition+as+a+public+health+innovation&amp;rft.volume=96&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=233-43&amp;rft.date=2006-02&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1470475%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F16380559&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2105%2FAJPH.2005.065409&amp;rft.aulast=Blocker&amp;rft.aufirst=JS&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1470475&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlcoholism"></span></span>
</li>
